<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Periods_in_English_history" title="Template:Periods in English history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Periods_in_English_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

<p><b>Roman Britain</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la"><i>Britannia</i></span> or, later, <i><span lang="la">Britanniae</span></i>, "the Britains") was the area of the island of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> that was governed by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, from <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> 43 to 410.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_Companion_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_Companion-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:129–131</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chronology_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chronology-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_invasions_of_Britain" title="Caesar's invasions of Britain">invaded Britain</a> in 55 and 54 <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> as part of his <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celts" title="Insular Celts">Britons</a> had been overrun or culturally assimilated by other <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celtic_tribes" title="List of Celtic tribes">tribes</a> during the <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">British Iron Age</a> and had been aiding Caesar's enemies. He received tribute, installed <a href="/wiki/Mandubracius" title="Mandubracius">a friendly king</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Trinovantes" title="Trinovantes">Trinovantes</a>, and returned to <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>. Planned invasions under <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> were called off in 34, 27, and 25 <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>. In <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> 40, <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a> assembled 200,000 men at the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">Channel</a>, only to have them gather seashells. Three years later, <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> directed four legions to <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">invade Britain</a> and restore <a href="/wiki/Verica" title="Verica">an exiled king</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Atrebates" title="Atrebates">Atrebates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Romans defeated the <a href="/wiki/Catuvellauni" title="Catuvellauni">Catuvellauni</a>, and then organized their conquests as the <b>Province of Britain</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la"><i>Provincia Britannia</i></span>). By the year 47, the Romans held the lands southeast of the <a href="/wiki/Fosse_Way" title="Fosse Way">Fosse Way</a>. Control over <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> was delayed by <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Caer_Caradoc" title="Battle of Caer Caradoc">reverses</a> and the effects of <a href="/wiki/Boudica" title="Boudica">Boudica</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boudica%27s_Rebellion" title="Boudica's Rebellion">rebellion</a>, but the Romans expanded steadily northward.</p>
<p>Under the 2nd-century emperors <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a>, <a href="#Occupation_and_retreat_from_southern_Scotland">two walls</a> were built to defend the Roman province from the <a href="/wiki/Caledonia" title="Caledonia">Caledonians</a>, whose realms in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a> were never directly controlled. Around 197, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Severan_Reforms" title="Severan Reforms">Severan Reforms</a> divided Britain into two provinces: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Britain" title="Upper Britain">Upper</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Britain" title="Lower Britain">Lower</a> (<i><span lang="la">Britannia Superior</span></i> and <i><span lang="la">Inferior</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diocletian_Reforms" title="Diocletian Reforms">Diocletian Reforms</a>, at the end of the 3rd century, Britannia was divided into four provinces under the direction of a <a href="/wiki/Vicarius" title="Vicarius">vicarius</a>, who administered the <span class="nowrap"><b>Diocese of the Britains</b></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> A fifth province, <a href="/wiki/Valentia_(Roman_Britain)" title="Valentia (Roman Britain)">Valentia</a>, is attested in the later 4th century. For much of the later period of the Roman occupation, Britannia was subject to <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarian</a> invasions and often came under the control of imperial <a href="/wiki/Roman_usurper" title="Roman usurper">usurpers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Imperial</a> pretenders. The final <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_withdrawal_from_Britain" title="Roman withdrawal from Britain">Roman withdrawal from Britain</a> occurred around 410; the native kingdoms are considered to have formed <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">Sub-Roman Britain</a> after that.</p>
<p>Following the conquest of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" title="Britons (Celtic people)">Britons</a>, a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Romano-British_culture" title="Romano-British culture">Romano-British culture</a> emerged as the Romans introduced improved <a href="/wiki/Roman_agriculture" title="Roman agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_urban_planning" title="Roman urban planning">urban planning</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_industry" title="Roman industry">industrial production</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_architecture" title="Roman architecture">architecture</a>. After the initial invasions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_historians" title="Roman historians">Roman historians</a> generally only mention Britain in passing. Thus, most present knowledge derives from <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> investigations and occasional <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphic</a> evidence lauding the Britannic achievements of an <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_Companion_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_Companion-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:46,323</sup> Over the centuries Roman citizens settled in Britain from many parts of the Empire; such as Italy, Spain, Syria and Algeria. However, the bulk of Roman soldiers that were based in garrisons all over the British Isles to fight constant guerilla insurrections came from present-day Northern France, Belgium and Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_-_Mary_Beard.27s_Ultimate_Rome:_Empire_Without_Limit_-_Episode_3_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_-_Mary_Beard.27s_Ultimate_Rome:_Empire_Without_Limit_-_Episode_3-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Roman_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_contact">Early contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Roman_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_invasions_of_Britain" title="Caesar's invasions of Britain">Caesar's invasions of Britain</a></div>
<p>Britain was known to the Classical world; the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginians</a> traded for <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornish</a> <a href="/wiki/Tin_mining_in_Britain" title="Tin mining in Britain">tin</a> in the 4th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Welsh_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh-9">[9]</a></sup> The Greeks referred to the <i><span lang="grc-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Cassiterides" title="Cassiterides">Cassiterides</a></span></i>, or "tin islands", and placed them near the west coast of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The Carthaginian sailor <a href="/wiki/Himilco" title="Himilco">Himilco</a> is said to have visited the island in the 5th century BC and the Greek explorer <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a> in the 4th. However, it was regarded as a place of mystery, with some writers refusing to believe it existed at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutarch_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plutarch-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first direct Roman contact was when <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> undertook two expeditions in 55 and 54 BC, as part of his conquest of <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, believing the Britons were helping the Gallic resistance. The first expedition was more a reconnaissance than a full invasion and gained a foothold on the coast of <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> but was unable to advance further because of storm damage to the ships and a lack of cavalry. Despite the military failure it was a political success, with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a> declaring a 20-day public holiday in Rome to honour the unprecedented achievement of obtaining hostages from Britain and defeating Belgian tribes on returning to the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second invasion involved a substantially larger force and Caesar coerced or invited many of the native Celtic tribes to pay tribute and give hostages in return for peace. A friendly local king, <a href="/wiki/Mandubracius" title="Mandubracius">Mandubracius</a>, was installed, and his rival, <a href="/wiki/Cassivellaunus" title="Cassivellaunus">Cassivellaunus</a>, was brought to terms. Hostages were taken, but historians disagree over whether any tribute was paid after Caesar returned to Gaul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Caesar conquered no territory and left no troops behind but he established <a href="/wiki/Patronage_in_ancient_Rome" title="Patronage in ancient Rome">clients</a> and brought Britain into Rome's sphere of influence. <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> planned invasions in 34, 27 and 25 BC, but circumstances were never favourable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and the relationship between Britain and Rome settled into one of diplomacy and trade. <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, writing late in Augustus's reign, claimed that taxes on trade brought in more annual revenue than any conquest could.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Archaeology shows that there was an increase in imported luxury goods in southeastern Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Branigan_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Branigan-16">[16]</a></sup> Strabo also mentions <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Kings" title="British Kings">British kings</a> who sent embassies to Augustus and Augustus's own <i><span lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Res_Gestae_Divi_Augusti" title="Res Gestae Divi Augusti">Res Gestae</a></span></i> refers to two British kings he received as refugees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> When some of <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>'s ships were carried to Britain in a storm during his campaigns in <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germany</a> in 16 AD, they came back with tales of monsters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rome appears to have encouraged a balance of power in southern Britain, supporting two powerful kingdoms: the <a href="/wiki/Catuvellauni" title="Catuvellauni">Catuvellauni</a>, ruled by the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Tasciovanus" title="Tasciovanus">Tasciovanus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Atrebates" title="Atrebates">Atrebates</a>, ruled by the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Commius" title="Commius">Commius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Creighton_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Creighton-19">[19]</a></sup> This policy was followed until 39 or 40 AD, when <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a> received an exiled member of the Catuvellaunian dynasty and planned an invasion of Britain that collapsed in farcical circumstances before it left Gaul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> successfully invaded in 43 AD, it was in aid of another fugitive British ruler, <a href="/wiki/Verica" title="Verica">Verica</a> of the Atrebates.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_invasion">Roman invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Roman_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Roman invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">Roman conquest of Britain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="491" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg/220px-Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg/330px-Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg/440px-Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Landing_of_the_Romans_on_the_Coast_of_Kent.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Landing of the Romans on the Coast of Kent (Cassell's History of England, Vol. I - anonymous author and artists).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The invasion force in AD 43 was led by <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Plautius" title="Aulus Plautius">Aulus Plautius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> but it is unclear how many <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">legions</a> were sent. The <i><span lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Augusta" title="Legio II Augusta">Legio II Augusta</a></span></i>, commanded by future emperor <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a>, was the only one directly attested to have taken part.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The <i><span lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Legio_IX_Hispana" title="Legio IX Hispana">IX Hispana</a></span></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> the <i><span lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Legio_XIV_Gemina" title="Legio XIV Gemina">XIV Gemina</a></span></i> (later styled <i><span lang="la">Martia Victrix</span></i>) and the <i><span lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Legio_XX_Valeria_Victrix" title="Legio XX Valeria Victrix">XX</a></span></i> (later styled <i><span lang="la">Valeria Victrix</span></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> are known to have served during the <a href="/wiki/Boudica" title="Boudica">Boudican Revolt</a> of 60/61, and were probably there since the initial invasion. However this is not certain because the <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Roman army</a> was flexible, with units being moved around whenever necessary. The <i><span lang="la">Legio IX Hispana</span></i> may have been permanently stationed with records showing it at Eboracum (York) in 71 and on a building inscription there dated 108, before being destroyed in the east of the Empire, possibly during the <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba revolt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The invasion was delayed by a troop mutiny until an imperial <a href="/wiki/Freedman" title="Freedman">freedman</a> persuaded them to overcome their fear of crossing the <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Ocean</a> and campaigning beyond the limits of the known world. They sailed in three divisions, and probably landed at <a href="/wiki/Richborough" title="Richborough">Richborough</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, although at least part of the force may have landed near <a href="/wiki/Fishbourne,_West_Sussex" title="Fishbourne, West Sussex">Fishbourne, West Sussex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2954" data-file-width="3084" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg/220px-British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg/330px-British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg/440px-British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British.coinage.Roman.invasion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Conquests under Aulus Plautius, focused on the commercially valuable southeast of Britain.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Catuvellauni and their allies were defeated in two battles: the first, assuming a Richborough landing, on the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Medway" title="Battle of the Medway">river Medway</a>, the second on the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">river Thames</a>. One of their leaders, <a href="/wiki/Togodumnus" title="Togodumnus">Togodumnus</a>, was killed, but his brother <a href="/wiki/Caratacus" title="Caratacus">Caratacus</a> survived to continue resistance elsewhere. Plautius halted at the Thames and sent for Claudius, who arrived with reinforcements, including artillery and elephants, for the final march to the Catuvellaunian capital, <a href="/wiki/Camulodunum" title="Camulodunum">Camulodunum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Colchester" title="Colchester">Colchester</a>). <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a> subdued the southwest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Claudius_Cogidubnus" title="Tiberius Claudius Cogidubnus">Cogidubnus</a> was set up as a friendly king of several territories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and treaties were made with tribes outside direct Roman control.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_rule_is_established">Roman rule is established</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Roman_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Roman rule is established">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Romano-British_culture" title="Romano-British culture">Romano-British culture</a></div>
<p>After capturing the south of the island, the Romans turned their attention to what is now Wales. The <a href="/wiki/Silures" title="Silures">Silures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ordovices" title="Ordovices">Ordovices</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deceangli" title="Deceangli">Deceangli</a> remained implacably opposed to the invaders and for the first few decades were the focus of Roman military attention, despite occasional minor revolts among Roman allies like the <a href="/wiki/Brigantes" title="Brigantes">Brigantes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iceni" title="Iceni">Iceni</a>. The Silures were led by <a href="/wiki/Caratacus" title="Caratacus">Caratacus</a>, and he carried out an effective <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla</a> campaign against Governor <a href="/wiki/Publius_Ostorius_Scapula" title="Publius Ostorius Scapula">Publius Ostorius Scapula</a>. Finally, in 51, Ostorius lured Caratacus into a set-piece battle and defeated him. The British leader sought refuge among the Brigantes, but their queen, <a href="/wiki/Cartimandua" title="Cartimandua">Cartimandua</a>, proved her loyalty by surrendering him to the Romans. He was brought as a captive to Rome, where a dignified speech he made during Claudius's triumph persuaded the emperor to spare his life. However, the Silures were still not pacified, and Cartimandua's ex-husband <a href="/wiki/Venutius" title="Venutius">Venutius</a> replaced Caratacus as the most prominent leader of British resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 60–61, while Governor <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Suetonius_Paulinus" title="Gaius Suetonius Paulinus">Gaius Suetonius Paulinus</a> was campaigning in Wales, the southeast of Britain rose in revolt under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Boudica" title="Boudica">Boudica</a>. Boudica was the widow of the recently deceased king of the Iceni, Prasutagus. The Roman historian Tacitus reports that Prasutagus had left a will leaving half his kingdom to <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> in the hope that the remainder would be left untouched. He was wrong. When his will was enforced, Rome responded by violently seizing the tribe's lands in full. Boudica protested. In consequence, Rome punished her and her daughters by flogging and rape. In response, the Iceni, joined by the <a href="/wiki/Trinovantes" title="Trinovantes">Trinovantes</a>, destroyed the Roman colony at Camulodunum (<a href="/wiki/Colchester" title="Colchester">Colchester</a>) and routed the part of the IXth Legion that was sent to relieve it. Suetonius Paulinus rode to <a href="/wiki/Londinium" title="Londinium">London</a>, the rebels' next target, but concluded it could not be defended. Abandoned, it was destroyed, as was <a href="/wiki/Verulamium" title="Verulamium">Verulamium</a> (St. Albans). Between seventy and eighty thousand people are said to have been killed in the three cities. But Suetonius regrouped with two of the three legions still available to him, chose a battlefield, and, despite being heavily outnumbered, defeated the rebels in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Watling_Street" title="Battle of Watling Street">Battle of Watling Street</a>. Boudica died not long afterwards, by self-administered poison or by illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> During this time, the Emperor Nero considered withdrawing Roman forces from Britain altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Templeborough_Roman_Fort_visualised_3D_flythrough_-_Rotherham.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Templeborough" title="Templeborough">Templeborough Roman fort</a> in Yorkshire. The reconstruction was created for Rotherham Museums and Galleries.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There was further turmoil in 69, the "<a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">Year of the Four Emperors</a>". As civil war raged in Rome, weak governors were unable to control the legions in Britain, and Venutius of the Brigantes seized his chance. The Romans had previously defended Cartimandua against him, but this time were unable to do so. Cartimandua was evacuated, and Venutius was left in control of the north of the country. After Vespasian secured the empire, his first two appointments as governor, <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Petillius_Cerialis" title="Quintus Petillius Cerialis">Quintus Petillius Cerialis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frontinus" title="Frontinus">Sextus Julius Frontinus</a>, took on the task of subduing the Brigantes and Silures respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Frontinus extended Roman rule to all of <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South Wales</a>, and initiated exploitation of the mineral resources, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gold_mining" title="Gold mining">gold mines</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dolaucothi_Gold_Mines" title="Dolaucothi Gold Mines">Dolaucothi</a>.</p>
<p>In the following years, the Romans conquered more of the island, increasing the size of Roman Britain. Governor <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Julius_Agricola" title="Gnaeus Julius Agricola">Gnaeus Julius Agricola</a>, father-in-law to the historian <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, conquered the <a href="/wiki/Ordovices" title="Ordovices">Ordovices</a> in 78. With the <i><span lang="la">XX Valeria Victrix</span></i> legion, Agricola defeated the <a href="/wiki/Caledonians" title="Caledonians">Caledonians</a> in 84 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mons_Graupius" title="Battle of Mons Graupius">Battle of Mons Graupius</a>, in northern Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This was the high-water mark of Roman territory in Britain: shortly after his victory, Agricola was recalled from Britain back to Rome, and the Romans retired to a more defensible line along the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Forth</a>–<a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Clyde</a> isthmus, freeing soldiers badly needed along other frontiers.</p>
<p>For much of the history of Roman Britain, a large number of soldiers were garrisoned on the island. This required that the emperor station a trusted senior man as governor of the province. As a result, many future emperors served as governors or legates in this province, including <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pertinax" title="Pertinax">Pertinax</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gordian_I" title="Gordian I">Gordian I</a>.</p>
